<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">North American Soccer League (1968–84)</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">This article is about the original national Division I soccer league that was active from 1968 to 1985. For the present-day Division II league, see <a href="/wiki/North_American_Soccer_League" title="North American Soccer League">North American Soccer League</a>.</div>

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:North_American_Soccer_League_1968-1974_logo.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="314" data-file-width="316" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c1/North_American_Soccer_League_1968-1974_logo.png/220px-North_American_Soccer_League_1968-1974_logo.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/c/c1/North_American_Soccer_League_1968-1974_logo.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:North_American_Soccer_League_1968-1974_logo.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Original logo of the NASL (1968–1974)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <b>North American Soccer League</b> (NASL) was the top-level major professional <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">soccer</a> <a href="/wiki/Sports_league" title="Sports league">league</a> in the United States and Canada that operated from 1968 to 1984. It was the first soccer league to be successful on a national scale in the United States. The league final was called the <a href="/wiki/Soccer_Bowl" title="Soccer Bowl">Soccer Bowl</a> from <a href="/wiki/1975_North_American_Soccer_League_season" title="1975 North American Soccer League season">1975</a> to <a href="/wiki/1983_North_American_Soccer_League_season" title="1983 North American Soccer League season">1983</a> and the Soccer Bowl Series in its final year, <a href="/wiki/1984_North_American_Soccer_League_season" title="1984 North American Soccer League season">1984</a>. The league was headed by Commissioner <a href="/wiki/Phil_Woosnam" title="Phil Woosnam">Phil Woosnam</a> from 1969 to 1983.</p>
<p>The league's popularity peaked in the late 1970s. The league averaged over 13,000 fans per game in each season from 1977 to 1983, and the league's matches were broadcast on network television from 1975 to 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> The league's most prominent team was the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Cosmos_(1970%E2%80%9385)" title="New York Cosmos (1970–85)">New York Cosmos</a>. During the mid-to-late 1970s, the Cosmos signed a number of the world's best players —<a href="/wiki/Pel%C3%A9" title="Pelé">Pelé</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franz_Beckenbauer" title="Franz Beckenbauer">Franz Beckenbauer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Alberto" title="Carlos Alberto">Carlos Alberto</a>— and the Cosmos averaged over 28,000 fans for each season from 1977 to 1982 while having three seasons of the average attendance topping 40,000 spectators per game. Other internationally well-known players in the league included <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Chinaglia" title="Giorgio Chinaglia">Giorgio Chinaglia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johann_Cruyff" title="Johann Cruyff">Johann Cruyff</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johan_Neeskens" title="Johan Neeskens">Johan Neeskens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gerd_M%C3%BCller" title="Gerd Müller">Gerd Müller</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Best" title="George Best">George Best</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rodney_Marsh_(footballer)" title="Rodney Marsh (footballer)">Rodney Marsh</a>.</p>
<p>The league additionally sanctioned <a href="/wiki/Indoor_soccer" title="Indoor soccer">indoor soccer</a> in 1971, and 1975–1976 and from 1979–1984.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_Soccer_League_(1968%E2%80%9384)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Founding">Founding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_Soccer_League_(1968%E2%80%9384)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Founding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The surprisingly large North American TV audience of over 1 million for the <a href="/wiki/1966_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1966 FIFA World Cup">1966 FIFA World Cup</a> and the resulting <a href="/wiki/Documentary_film" title="Documentary film">documentary film</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/List_of_official_FIFA_World_Cup_films" title="List of official FIFA World Cup films">Goal!</a></i>, led American sports investors to believe there was an untapped market for the sport in the U.S. and Canada. In 1967, two professional soccer leagues started in the United States: the <a href="/wiki/FIFA" title="FIFA">FIFA</a>-sanctioned <a href="/wiki/United_Soccer_Association" title="United Soccer Association">United Soccer Association</a>, which consisted of entire European and South American teams brought to the U.S. and given local names, and the unsanctioned <a href="/wiki/National_Professional_Soccer_League_(1967)" title="National Professional Soccer League (1967)">National Professional Soccer League</a>. While the USA had FIFA sanction, the foreign teams which were rebranded as American for the summer 1967 season viewed the league as little more than a training exercise for their off-season, and most did not field their best players. The NPSL had a two-year national television contract in the U.S. with the <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a> <a href="/wiki/Television_network" title="Television network">television network</a>...referees were instructed to whistle fouls and delay play to allow CBS to insert commercials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kenn.com_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-kenn.com-3">[3]</a></sup> The ratings for matches were unacceptable even by weekend daytime standards and the arrangement with CBS was soon terminated. <a href="/wiki/Bill_MacPhail" title="Bill MacPhail">Bill MacPhail</a>, head of <a href="/wiki/CBS_Sports" title="CBS Sports">CBS Sports</a>, attributed NPSL's lack of TV appeal to empty stadiums with few fans, and to undistinguished foreign players who were unfamiliar to American soccer fans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sportsillustrated.cnn.com_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-sportsillustrated.cnn.com-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>The two leagues merged on December 7, 1967 to form the North American Soccer League (NASL). NASL began the <a href="/wiki/1968_North_American_Soccer_League_season" title="1968 North American Soccer League season">1968 season</a> with 17 of the 22 teams that had participated during the 1967 season, folding five redundant teams in cities where both USA and NPSL had operated. The teams relied mostly on foreign talent, including the Brazilian <a href="/wiki/Vav%C3%A1" title="Vavá">Vavá</a>, one of the leading scorers of the <a href="/wiki/1958_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1958 FIFA World Cup">1958</a> and <a href="/wiki/1962_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1962 FIFA World Cup">1962 World Cups</a>. International friendlies included victories against Pelé's <a href="/wiki/Santos_FC" title="Santos FC">Santos</a> and against English champions <a href="/wiki/Manchester_City_F.C." title="Manchester City F.C.">Manchester City</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sover1968_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-sover1968-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Though the league had a few successes, the league had significant problems gaining acceptance in the American sports community. The 17 teams included only 30 North American players.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sover1968_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-sover1968-5">[5]</a></sup> The expenses of high salaries for foreign players and renting of large stadiums, coupled with low attendances, resulted in every team losing money in 1968, and investors quickly pulled the plug after their year's commitment ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sover1968_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-sover1968-5">[5]</a></sup> At the end of the year, CBS pulled its TV contract, and all but five of the teams folded.The league moved its offices to a basement in Atlanta's stadium, and at the end of the eight game 1969 season, the league declared Kansas City the league champions without a playoff, and the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Bays" title="Baltimore Bays">Baltimore Bays</a> announced they would fold. It appeared top-tier professional soccer would not survive in North America.</p>
<p>Desperate to keep the league afloat, the league approached two <a href="/wiki/American_Soccer_League_(1933%E2%80%9383)" title="American Soccer League (1933–83)">American Soccer League</a> teams, the <a href="/wiki/Rochester_Lancers" title="Rochester Lancers">Rochester Lancers</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Darts" title="Washington Darts">Washington Darts</a> about transferring to the NASL. Despite coming from the ASL (which had a nearly 40 year history as a semi-pro league), the two teams were immediately the most successful, and won their respective divisions. Rochester beat Washington in a two game final, and the league survived.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/1971_North_American_Soccer_League_season" title="1971 North American Soccer League season">1971</a>, NASL added three teams—the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Cosmos_(1970%E2%80%9385)" title="New York Cosmos (1970–85)">New York Cosmos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Olympique" title="Montreal Olympique">Montreal Olympique</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toronto_Metros-Croatia" title="Toronto Metros-Croatia">Toronto Metros</a>—each of which paid a $25,000 expansion fee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Tornado" title="Dallas Tornado">Dallas Tornado</a> won the title after a number of multiple overtime playoff games, including a 173 minute marathon against Rochester.</p>
<p>The NASL realized it needed to sell to North Americans the sport of soccer, which was still foreign to most people. The league modified the rules in an attempt to make the game more exciting, and comprehensible, to the average sports fan. These changes included a clock that counted time down to zero as was typical of other timed American sports, rather than upwards to 90 minutes as was traditional. NASL implemented in <a href="/wiki/1972_North_American_Soccer_League_season" title="1972 North American Soccer League season">1972</a> a 35 yd (32 m) line for offside<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> (a rule change designed to reduce prevalent offside traps) rather than the usual half-way line, a rule change which FIFA tolerated until 1982.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> NASL introduced a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penalty_shootout_(football)" title="Penalty shootout (football)">penalty shootout</a> in <a href="/wiki/1974_North_American_Soccer_League_season" title="1974 North American Soccer League season">1974</a> to decide matches that ended in a draw. The league also carried over the points system used by the NPSL in the previous year in which teams were awarded 6 points for win and 3 for a draw, plus up to 3 bonus points for each goal scored. On five occasions this nontraditional system gave the regular season title to a team other than the one with the best record (most notably in 1980 when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seattle_Sounders" title="Seattle Sounders">Seattle Sounders</a> set the league record of 25 games, yet the Cosmos were awarded the title with one fewer win).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interest_begins_to_grow">Interest begins to grow</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_Soccer_League_(1968%E2%80%9384)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Interest begins to grow">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The NASL of the early 1970s was, to a large extent, a <a href="/wiki/Semi-professional" title="Semi-professional">semi-pro</a> league, with many of the players holding other jobs.</p>
<p>On September 3, 1973, <i><a href="/wiki/Sports_Illustrated" title="Sports Illustrated">Sports Illustrated</a></i> featured a soccer player on its cover for the first time — <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Atoms" title="Philadelphia Atoms">Philadelphia Atoms</a> goalkeeper <a href="/wiki/Bob_Rigby" title="Bob Rigby">Bob Rigby</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tdifh.blogspot.com_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-tdifh.blogspot.com-10">[10]</a></sup> SI profiled the Philadelphia Atoms victory in the <a href="/wiki/NASL_Final_1973" title="NASL Final 1973">NASL championship</a>, the first time an American expansion sports team won a title in its first season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tdifh.blogspot.com_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-tdifh.blogspot.com-10">[10]</a></sup> Philadelphia averaged 11,500 fans in <a href="/wiki/1973_North_American_Soccer_League_season" title="1973 North American Soccer League season">1973</a>, the first time since 1967 that any North American professional soccer team had averaged over 10,000 fans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasljerseys1_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasljerseys1-11">[11]</a></sup> The cover title declared "Soccer Goes American", as Philadelphia had started six Americans in the championship match. Despite the "Soccer Goes American" title, however, in no season after 1974 did any American player win the MVP award or finish as league top scorer, as the mid-1970s saw an influx of foreign talent. SI predicted continued success for the Philadelphia Atoms, but the Atoms dissolved in 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tdifh.blogspot.com_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-tdifh.blogspot.com-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>NASL's average attendance had grown steadily from a low of 2,930 in 1969 to 7,770 in 1974, and by 1974 four teams were averaging over 10,000 attendance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasljerseys1_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasljerseys1-11">[11]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/NASL_Final_1974" title="NASL Final 1974">1974 NASL Championship game</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Aztecs" title="Los Angeles Aztecs">Los Angeles Aztecs</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Miami_Toros" title="Miami Toros">Miami Toros</a> was televised live on CBS, the first national broadcast of a pro soccer match in the United States since 1968.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/1974_North_American_Soccer_League_season" title="1974 North American Soccer League season">1974</a> and <a href="/wiki/1975_North_American_Soccer_League_season" title="1975 North American Soccer League season">1975 seasons</a> saw rapid expansion for NASL. In 1974, eight new teams paid the $75,000 franchise fee and joined the league, although two existing teams folded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sover1974_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-sover1974-13">[13]</a></sup> The 1974 expansion saw teams on the west coast, giving NASL a national presence for the first time. The west coast expansion was a success, with three of the teams — <a href="/wiki/San_Jose_Earthquakes_(1974%E2%80%9388)" title="San Jose Earthquakes (1974–88)">San Jose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seattle_Sounders_(1974%E2%80%9383)" title="Seattle Sounders (1974–83)">Seattle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Whitecaps_(1974%E2%80%9384)" title="Vancouver Whitecaps (1974–84)">Vancouver</a> — averaging over 10,000 fans in 1974.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sover1974_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-sover1974-13">[13]</a></sup> In 1975, five more franchises were added. Two of these five additions — Chicago and Hartford — were in cities that had successful franchises in the Division II <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Soccer_League_(1933%E2%80%931983)" title="American Soccer League (1933–1983)">American Soccer League</a>, which at the time saw itself as a potential challenger to NASL as the U.S.'s top professional soccer league.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sover1975_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-sover1975-14">[14]</a></sup> The expansions of 1974 and 1975 meant that NASL had grown from 9 teams in 1973 to 20 teams by 1975.</p>
<p>The 1975 season saw the signing of internationally known players, including Portuguese star <a href="/wiki/Eus%C3%A9bio" title="Eusébio">Eusébio</a> (who Rhode Island of the Division II ASL had tried to sign),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sover1975_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-sover1975-14">[14]</a></sup> and former England goalkeeper <a href="/wiki/Peter_Bonetti" title="Peter Bonetti">Peter Bonetti</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pel.C3.A9_and_the_New_York_Cosmos">Pelé and the New York Cosmos</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_Soccer_League_(1968%E2%80%9384)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Pelé and the New York Cosmos">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pel%C3%A9" title="Pelé">Pelé</a>'s arrival created a media sensation and overnight transformed the fortunes of <a href="/wiki/Soccer_in_the_United_States" title="Soccer in the United States">soccer in the United States</a>. From the moment he signed his contract at the <a href="/wiki/21_Club" title="21 Club">21 Club</a> on June 10, 1975 in front of a crush of ecstatic worldwide media, Pelé's every move was followed, bringing attention and credibility to soccer in America. The <a href="/wiki/New_York_Cosmos_(1970%E2%80%9385)" title="New York Cosmos (1970–85)">New York Cosmos</a>' home attendance tripled in just half the season Pelé was there, and on the road the Cosmos also played in front of huge crowds that came to watch Pelé play.</p>
<p>Pelé's arrival resulted in greater TV exposure for the Cosmos and for the league overall. Ten million people tuned in to watch CBS' live broadcast of Pelé's debut match—a record American TV audience for soccer—with the Cosmos on June 15, 1975 against the <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Tornado" title="Dallas Tornado">Dallas Tornado</a> at <a href="/wiki/Downing_Stadium" title="Downing Stadium">Downing Stadium</a> in New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kenn.com_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-kenn.com-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sportsillustrated.cnn.com_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-sportsillustrated.cnn.com-4">[4]</a></sup> CBS also televised another Cosmos match plus the <a href="/wiki/Soccer_Bowl_%2775" title="Soccer Bowl '75">1975 Soccer Bowl</a> championship match, and in 1976 <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a> signed a contract to broadcast matches during the <a href="/wiki/1976_North_American_Soccer_League_season" title="1976 North American Soccer League season">1976 season</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kenn.com_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-kenn.com-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sportsillustrated.cnn.com_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-sportsillustrated.cnn.com-4">[4]</a></sup> By 1976, NASL was being picked up by the mainstream media, with the sports pages of newspapers covering NASL.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sportsillustrated.cnn.com_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-sportsillustrated.cnn.com-4">[4]</a></sup> The NASL was shown on the <a href="/wiki/TVS_Television_Network" title="TVS Television Network">TVS network</a> during <a href="/wiki/1977_North_American_Soccer_League_season" title="1977 North American Soccer League season">1977</a> and <a href="/wiki/1978_North_American_Soccer_League_season" title="1978 North American Soccer League season">1978</a>, although some games were tape delayed or not carried in certain markets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kenn.com_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-kenn.com-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The biggest club in the league and the organization's bellwether was the Cosmos, who drew upwards of 40,000 fans per game at their height while, along with <a href="/wiki/Pel%C3%A9" title="Pelé">Pelé</a> (Brazil), another aging superstar <a href="/wiki/Franz_Beckenbauer" title="Franz Beckenbauer">Franz Beckenbauer</a> (Germany) played for them. Although both well past their prime by the time they joined the NASL, the two were considered to have previously been the best attacking (offensive) (Pelé) and defensive (Beckenbauer) players in the world.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Giants_Stadium" title="Giants Stadium">Giants Stadium</a> sold out (73,000+) their <a href="/wiki/Soccer_Bowl_%2778" title="Soccer Bowl '78">1978 championship</a> win. However, the overall average attendance of the entire league never reached 15,000, with some clubs averaging less than 5,000.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_and_star_players">Expansion and star players</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_Soccer_League_(1968%E2%80%9384)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Expansion and star players">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Los Angeles Aztecs signed <a href="/wiki/Manchester_United_F.C." title="Manchester United F.C.">Manchester United</a> star <a href="/wiki/George_Best" title="George Best">George Best</a> in 1976. NASL had been trying to persuade Best to come to America and place him in a major media market, but once the New York Cosmos had signed Pelé, Los Angeles was the logical placement for Best.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ussoccerplayers.com_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-ussoccerplayers.com-15">[15]</a></sup> Best was traded to <a href="/wiki/Fort_Lauderdale_Strikers_(1977%E2%80%9383)" title="Fort Lauderdale Strikers (1977–83)">Fort Lauderdale</a> in 1978, and in 1979 Los Angeles signed its next big star, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johann_Cruyff" title="Johann Cruyff">Johann Cruyff</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ussoccerplayers.com_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-ussoccerplayers.com-15">[15]</a></sup> Cruyff was an instant success, doubling the team's attendance, and winning the league's MVP award.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sports_Illustrated_1980_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sports_Illustrated_1980-16">[16]</a></sup> LA also brought in a new head coach from 1979–1980, <a href="/wiki/Rinus_Michels" title="Rinus Michels">Rinus Michels</a>, who had coached <a href="/wiki/AFC_Ajax" title="AFC Ajax">Ajax Amsterdam</a>, <a href="/wiki/FC_Barcelona" title="FC Barcelona">Barcelona</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands_national_football_team" title="Netherlands national football team">Dutch national team</a>, the man credited with the invention of the Dutch playing style of "<a href="/wiki/Total_Football" title="Total Football">Total Football</a>" in the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uefa_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-uefa-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Minnesota_Kicks" title="Minnesota Kicks">Minnesota Kicks</a> were established in 1976 and quickly became one of the league's more popular teams, drawing an average attendance of 23,120 fans per game in 1976 to the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Stadium" title="Metropolitan Stadium">Metropolitan Stadium</a> in the suburbs of Minneapolis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The Kicks won their division four years in a row from 1976–79, drawing over 23,000 fans in each of those four seasons (peaking at 32,775 in 1977).</p>
<p>After LA, Cruyff then moved on to the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Diplomats" title="Washington Diplomats">Washington Diplomats</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Washington_Diplomats" title="Washington Diplomats">Washington Diplomats</a> had been purchased by Madison Square Garden Corp. and its Chairman <a href="/wiki/Sonny_Werblin" title="Sonny Werblin">Sonny Werblin</a> in October 1978. Cruyff's presence was a huge boost, as was <a href="/wiki/Wim_Jansen" title="Wim Jansen">Wim Jansen</a>, a midfielder who had played for the Netherlands at the <a href="/wiki/1974_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1974 FIFA World Cup">1974</a> and <a href="/wiki/1978_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1978 FIFA World Cup">1978</a> World Cups. For the 1980 season, the Diplomats attendance was 19,205 spectators per match.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-funwhileitlasted.net_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-funwhileitlasted.net-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite NASL's apparent success, of NASL's 18 teams in 1977, six were considered franchises that needed to be relocated, bought out, or folded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> A planning committee of owners issued a report recommending that NASL strengthen its existing teams, and limit expansion to two franchises for 1978, with one additional franchise per year for the following years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes1982_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes1982-22">[22]</a></sup> Despite this recommendation, NASL brought in six new teams at $3 million per team, raising the league's teams from 18 to 24 for the <a href="/wiki/1978_North_American_Soccer_League_season" title="1978 North American Soccer League season">1978 season</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/San_Diego_Sockers_(1978%E2%80%9396)" title="San Diego Sockers (1978–96)">San Diego Sockers</a> President Jack Daley later described NASL's boom years of the late 1970s: "It became fashionable to chase the Cosmos. Everyone had to have a Pelé. Coaches went around the world on talent searches, forcing the prices up."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sports_Illustrated_1984_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sports_Illustrated_1984-23">[23]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Portland_Timbers_(1975%E2%80%9382)" title="Portland Timbers (1975–82)">Portland Timbers</a> tripled their team payroll from 1979 to 1980 in an effort to keep up with the league average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>The league began a college draft in 1972 in an attempt to increase the number of U.S.- and Canadian-born players in the league. The foreign image of soccer was not helped, however, by a league that brought in many older, high-profile foreign players, and frequently left Americans on the bench. This effort was often doubly futile, as while many of the foreign players were perhaps "big names" in their home countries, almost none of them qualified as such in North America, and they quickly absorbed most of the available payroll, such as it was, which could have otherwise been used to pay North American players better. As of 1979, NASL rules required that each squad start two U.S. or Canadian players—often a goalkeeper and an outside defender<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-24">[24]</a></sup>—and that each 17-man roster carry six native players.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sports_Ilustrated_1979_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sports_Ilustrated_1979-25">[25]</a></sup> The U.S. had lacked sufficient quality youth soccer programs in the 1950s, resulting in the dearth of U.S.-born talent in NASL in the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sports_Ilustrated_1979_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sports_Ilustrated_1979-25">[25]</a></sup> NASL suffered a minor blow with a players strike at the start of the <a href="/wiki/1979_North_American_Soccer_League_season" title="1979 North American Soccer League season">1979 season</a>, but the strike was honored by only one third of the players and lasted only five days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1980, the minimum number of U.S. and Canadian starters was raised to three.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sports_Ilustrated_1979_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sports_Ilustrated_1979-25">[25]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1980_North_American_Soccer_League_season" title="1980 North American Soccer League season">1980 season</a> was referred to as "the year of the North American player" with a renewed emphasis on "native players."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-24">[24]</a></sup> With the increased requirements for teams to field U.S. and Canadian players, demand for quality native players boomed, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jim_McAlister_(American_soccer)" title="Jim McAlister (American soccer)">Jim McAlister</a> setting a transfer record for an American player at $200,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the end of the 1970s, NASL seemed poised for moderate success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sports_Illustrated_1980_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sports_Illustrated_1980-16">[16]</a></sup> The 1979 season had seen attendance increase by 8%. ABC televised several matches during the 1979 and 1980 seasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> An apparent era of stability seemed to have arrived, with the 1980 season expecting no planned expansion, relocations or failed teams among its 24 franchises, and with most rosters remaining relatively stable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sports_Illustrated_1980_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sports_Illustrated_1980-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Financial_problems_and_contraction">Financial problems and contraction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_Soccer_League_(1968%E2%80%9384)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Financial problems and contraction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the close of the 1980 season, NASL's woes were beginning to mount, as NASL was feeling the effects of over-expansion, the economic recession, and disputes with the players union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-28">[28]</a></sup> In the early 1980s the U.S. economy went into the doldrums, with unemployment reaching 10.8% in 1982, its highest level since World War II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Urquhart_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Urquhart-29">[29]</a></sup> NASL's owners, who were losing money, were not immune from the broader economy.</p>
<p>Perhaps most troubling of all, NASL owners were spending sums on player salaries that could not be covered by league revenue. Whereas NFL owners in 1980 were spending on average 40% of the team's budget on player salaries, NASL owners were averaging over 70% of their budget on player salaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-28">[28]</a></sup> The Cosmos in particular, owned by <a href="/wiki/Warner_Communications" title="Warner Communications">Warner Communications</a>, were spending lavish sums on player salaries, and while other teams—such as Los Angeles, <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville_Tea_Men" title="Jacksonville Tea Men">Jacksonville</a>, Portland, <a href="/wiki/Toronto_Blizzard_(1971%E2%80%9384)" title="Toronto Blizzard (1971–84)">Toronto</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Manic" title="Montreal Manic">Montreal</a>—that were owned by major corporations could keep up with the Cosmos, owners without deep pockets could not keep pace with the spending levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-28">[28]</a></sup> Owners spent millions on aging stars to try to match the success of the Cosmos, and lost significant amounts of money in doing so.</p>
<p>Another headache for NASL was competition from the resurgent <a href="/wiki/Major_Indoor_Soccer_League_(1978%E2%80%9392)" title="Major Indoor Soccer League (1978–92)">Major Indoor Soccer League</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-homepages.sover.net_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-homepages.sover.net-30">[30]</a></sup> The MISL began during the <a href="/wiki/1978%E2%80%9379_Major_Indoor_Soccer_League_season" title="1978–79 Major Indoor Soccer League season">1978–79 season</a>, grew quickly, and by the early 1980s MISL was averaging over 8,000 fans per game. MISL's growth meant that throughout the early 1980s the NASL and the MISL engaged in a bidding war for U.S. based soccer players, putting further pressure on league salaries and heightening NASL's financial problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-homepages.sover.net_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-homepages.sover.net-30">[30]</a></sup> In an effort to vie for MISL's expanding audiences, the NASL operated an indoor soccer league from <a href="/wiki/1979%E2%80%9380_NASL_Indoor_season" title="1979–80 NASL Indoor season">1979–80</a> to <a href="/wiki/1981%E2%80%9382_NASL_Indoor_season" title="1981–82 NASL Indoor season">1981–82</a> and in <a href="/wiki/1983%E2%80%9384_NASL_Indoor_season" title="1983–84 NASL Indoor season">1983–84</a>.</p>
<p>As a result, the league ran a collective deficit in 1980 of about $30 million, with each team losing money.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-28">[28]</a></sup> The San Diego Sockers lost $10 million from 1978 to 1983, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tulsa_Roughnecks_(1978%E2%80%931984)" title="Tulsa Roughnecks (1978–1984)">Tulsa</a> lost $8 million from 1980 to 1983.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sports_Illustrated_1984_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sports_Illustrated_1984-23">[23]</a></sup> The Washington Diplomats folded in November 1980, after owners MSG Corp. lost a rumored $5 million on the team in 1979 and 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-funwhileitlasted.net_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-funwhileitlasted.net-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>NASL had also decided to sell TV advertising locally, instead of recruiting national sponsors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-articles.latimes.com_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-articles.latimes.com-31">[31]</a></sup> During the 1980 offseason, the NASL Players' Association was in dispute with the league over projected payments for the indoor season, causing the players to file a lawsuit against the league.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/1981_North_American_Soccer_League_season" title="1981 North American Soccer League season">1981 season</a> was even worse for the league, with the league's 24 teams again running a collective deficit of $30 million and every team losing money.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google6924_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-google6924-32">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ted_Turner" title="Ted Turner">Ted Turner</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Atlanta_Chiefs" title="Atlanta Chiefs">Atlanta Chiefs</a> lost $7 million, the Minnesota Kicks lost $2.5 million, the <a href="/wiki/Calgary_Boomers" title="Calgary Boomers">Calgary Boomers</a> lost over $2 million, and <a href="/wiki/Lamar_Hunt" title="Lamar Hunt">Lamar Hunt</a>'s Dallas Tornado had lost $1 million annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google6924_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-google6924-32">[32]</a></sup> At the close of the 1981 season five teams folded, with another two teams—the L.A. Aztecs and Minnesota Kicks—later folding during the 1981-82 offseason after failing to find buyers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google6924_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-google6924-32">[32]</a></sup> NASL shrank from 21 teams to 14.</p>
<p>Many of these new owners were not soccer savvy, and once the perceived popularity started to decline, they got out as quickly as they got in. Over-expansion without sufficient vetting of ownership groups was a huge factor in the death of the league.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-articles.latimes.com_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-articles.latimes.com-31">[31]</a></sup> Once the league started growing, new franchises were awarded quickly, and it doubled in size in a few years, peaking at 24 teams. Many have suggested that cash-starved existing owners longed for their share of the expansion fee charged of new owners, even though <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes Magazine</a></i> reported this amount as being only $100,000.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_and_demise">Decline and demise</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_Soccer_League_(1968%E2%80%9384)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Decline and demise">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>With the NASL declining rapidly in the early 1980s and losing many franchises, NASL made several changes in an attempt to keep the league going. <a href="/wiki/Phil_Woosnam" title="Phil Woosnam">Phil Woosnam</a>, who had served as NASL Commissioner since 1969 and had been a strong proponent of expansion during the 1970s, was removed by the league's 14 owners in April 1982 by a reported 11-3 vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes1982_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes1982-22">[22]</a></sup> NASL tried to help bring the 1986 World Cup to the United States after Colombia withdrew from its commitment to host, but FIFA decided in 1983 to award the hosting of the <a href="/wiki/1986_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1986 FIFA World Cup">1986 FIFA World Cup</a> to Mexico, rather than the U.S. In early 1984, NASL reached a collective bargaining agreement with the NASL Players Association that included a $825,000 salary cap to be achieved by annual 10% reductions, and a reduction in roster sizes from 28 to 19.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sports_Illustrated_1984_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sports_Illustrated_1984-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>The league lasted until the <a href="/wiki/1984_North_American_Soccer_League_season" title="1984 North American Soccer League season">1984 NASL season</a>. On March 28, 1985, the NASL suspended operations for the 1985 season, when only the <a href="/wiki/Minnesota_Strikers" title="Minnesota Strikers">Minnesota Strikers</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toronto_Blizzard_(NASL)" title="Toronto Blizzard (NASL)">Toronto Blizzard</a> were interested in playing. At the time, the league planned to relaunch in 1986.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, four NASL teams (<a href="/wiki/Chicago_Sting" title="Chicago Sting">Chicago Sting</a>, Minnesota Strikers, New York Cosmos, and San Diego Sockers) joined the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Major_Soccer_League" title="Major Soccer League">Major Indoor Soccer League</a> for its <a href="/wiki/1984%E2%80%9385_Major_Indoor_Soccer_League_season" title="1984–85 Major Indoor Soccer League season">1984–85 season</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_Bay_Earthquakes" title="Golden Bay Earthquakes">Golden Bay Earthquakes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tampa_Bay_Rowdies_(1975%E2%80%9393)" title="Tampa Bay Rowdies (1975–93)">Tampa Bay Rowdies</a> managed to survive as independent franchises until they joined the <a href="/wiki/Western_Soccer_Alliance" title="Western Soccer Alliance">WSA</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Indoor_Soccer_Association" title="American Indoor Soccer Association">AISL</a> respectively. The Rowdies were the last surviving NASL franchise to play outdoor soccer, lasting until February 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The Sockers' were the final league franchise to dissolve. They survived playing exclusively indoor soccer until 1996.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_Soccer_League_(1968%E2%80%9384)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/United_States_men%27s_national_soccer_team" title="United States men's national soccer team">United States</a>' early elimination in <a href="/wiki/1982_FIFA_World_Cup_qualification" title="1982 FIFA World Cup qualification">1982 World Cup qualifying</a>, American manager <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walt_Chyzowych" title="Walt Chyzowych">Walt Chyzowych</a> stated the NASL had failed to offer much of a foundation for his team, since the league had largely failed to develop American players.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wangerin_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wangerin-35">[35]</a></sup> On the other hand, <a href="/wiki/Canada_men%27s_national_soccer_team" title="Canada men's national soccer team">Canada</a> nearly qualified for the 1982 World Cup with a squad exclusively made up of NASL players.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Although the NASL ultimately failed, it did introduce soccer to the North American sports scene on a large scale for the first time, and was a major contributing factor in soccer becoming one of the most popular sports among American youth. On July 4, 1988, FIFA awarded the hosting of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1994_World_Cup" title="1994 World Cup">1994 World Cup</a> to the United States. NASL has also provided lessons for its successor <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Soccer" title="Major League Soccer">Major League Soccer</a>, which has taken precautions against such problems, particularly a philosophy of financial restraint (mainstream American sports, by the time of MLS' startup in 1996, had adopted financial restraint rules, which MLS adopted). American college and high school soccer still use some NASL-style rules (with shortened halves, although the time does stop for certain reasons).</p>
<p>18 of the 22 players on the <a href="/wiki/1986_FIFA_World_Cup_squads" title="1986 FIFA World Cup squads">Canadian squad</a> at the <a href="/wiki/1986_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1986 FIFA World Cup">1986 World Cup</a> were former NASL players. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_men%27s_national_soccer_team" title="United States men's national soccer team">United States</a> did not have any former NASL players on their squad at the <a href="/wiki/1990_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1990 FIFA World Cup">1990 World Cup</a> but had three on the 1994 team (<a href="/wiki/Fernando_Clavijo" title="Fernando Clavijo">Fernando Clavijo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hugo_P%C3%A9rez_(soccer)" title="Hugo Pérez (soccer)">Hugo Pérez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roy_Wegerle" title="Roy Wegerle">Roy Wegerle</a>) and one on the <a href="/wiki/1998_FIFA_World_Cup_squads" title="1998 FIFA World Cup squads">1998 team</a> (Wegerle).</p>
<p>Several of the NASL team names have been reused for teams in later soccer leagues. The <a href="/wiki/Portland_Timbers" title="Portland Timbers">Portland Timbers</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Jose_Earthquakes" title="San Jose Earthquakes">San Jose Earthquakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seattle_Sounders_FC" title="Seattle Sounders FC">Seattle Sounders</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Whitecaps_FC" title="Vancouver Whitecaps FC">Vancouver Whitecaps</a> names have all been used for successor teams in <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Soccer" title="Major League Soccer">Major League Soccer</a>, with other team names (<a href="/wiki/New_York_Cosmos_(2010)" title="New York Cosmos (2010)">New York Cosmos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tampa_Bay_Rowdies" title="Tampa Bay Rowdies">Tampa Bay Rowdies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fort_Lauderdale_Strikers" title="Fort Lauderdale Strikers">Fort Lauderdale Strikers</a>) used in the <a href="/wiki/North_American_Soccer_League" title="North American Soccer League">Division II NASL</a> that began play in 2011. Two of the oldest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Local_derby" title="Local derby">derbies</a> in North American professional soccer <i>(<a href="/wiki/Portland_Timbers%E2%80%93Seattle_Sounders_rivalry" title="Portland Timbers–Seattle Sounders rivalry">Portland–Seattle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fort_Lauderdale%E2%80%93Tampa_Bay_rivalry" title="Fort Lauderdale–Tampa Bay rivalry">Fort Lauderdale–Tampa Bay</a>)</i> began in the NASL of the 1970s, and continue today via successor clubs.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="NASL_indoor">NASL indoor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_Soccer_League_(1968%E2%80%9384)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: NASL indoor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The NASL first staged an indoor tournament in <a href="/wiki/1971_NASL_Professional_Hoc-Soc_Tournament" title="1971 NASL Professional Hoc-Soc Tournament">1971</a> at the <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Arena" title="St. Louis Arena">St. Louis Arena</a> with a $2,800 purse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> After a couple of years of experimenting, including a three-city tour by the <a href="/wiki/CSKA_Moscow" title="CSKA Moscow">Red Army</a> team from Moscow in 1974, the league again staged tournaments in <a href="/wiki/1975_NASL_Indoor_tournament" title="1975 NASL Indoor tournament">1975</a> and <a href="/wiki/1976_NASL_Indoor_tournament" title="1976 NASL Indoor tournament">1976</a>. For many years Tampa Bay owner <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_W._Strawbridge,_Jr." title="George W. Strawbridge, Jr.">George W. Strawbridge, Jr.</a> continually lobbied his fellow owners to start up a winter indoor season, but was always stone-walled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> For several years, his Rowdies and several other teams used winter indoor "friendlies" as part of their training and build-up to the outdoor season. In the meantime, pressed by the rival <a href="/wiki/Major_Indoor_Soccer_League_(1978%E2%80%9392)" title="Major Indoor Soccer League (1978–92)">Major Indoor Soccer League</a> (MISL), which inaugurated play in 1978, the four-team <a href="/wiki/1979_NASL_Budweiser_Indoor_Soccer_Invitational" title="1979 NASL Budweiser Indoor Soccer Invitational">NASL Budweiser Invitational</a> was played over two days in January 1979 before a full arena.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The NASL finally started a full indoor league schedule, a 12-game season with 10 teams, in <a href="/wiki/1979%E2%80%9380_NASL_Indoor_season" title="1979–80 NASL Indoor season">1979–80</a>. For the <a href="/wiki/1980%E2%80%9381_NASL_Indoor_season" title="1980–81 NASL Indoor season">1980–81 season</a>, the number of teams playing indoor soccer increased to 19 and the schedule went to 18 games. The schedule remained at 18 games, but the teams participating decreased to 13 for the <a href="/wiki/1981%E2%80%9382_NASL_Indoor_season" title="1981–82 NASL Indoor season">1981–82 season</a>. The league canceled the 1982–83 indoor season and three teams (Chicago, Golden Bay, and San Diego) played in the <a href="/wiki/Major_Indoor_Soccer_League_(1978%E2%80%9392)" title="Major Indoor Soccer League (1978–92)">MISL</a> for that season. Four other teams (Ft. Lauderdale, Montreal, Tampa Bay and Tulsa) competed in a short <a href="/wiki/1983_NASL_Grand_Prix_of_Indoor_Soccer" title="1983 NASL Grand Prix of Indoor Soccer">NASL Grand Prix of Indoor Soccer Tournament</a> in early 1983.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The NASL indoor season returned for <a href="/wiki/1983%E2%80%9384_NASL_Indoor_season" title="1983–84 NASL Indoor season">1983–84</a> with only seven teams but a 32-game schedule.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="NASL_champions">NASL champions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_Soccer_League_(1968%E2%80%9384)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: NASL champions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Teams">Teams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_Soccer_League_(1968%E2%80%9384)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Teams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl>
<dt>Legend</dt>
</dl>
<p><i><span style="background-color:#B3B7FF; color:; border:1px solid darkgray; text-align:center;">    </span> – existed before 1968 NASL formation. <span style="background-color:#ccffcc; color:; border:1px solid darkgray; text-align:center;">    </span> – continued after 1984 NASL demise. <span style="background-color:#FFCCCC; color:; border:1px solid darkgray; text-align:center;">    </span> – existed before 1968 and after 1984.</i></p>

<p><i>*Operated as <a href="/wiki/Toronto_Croatia" title="Toronto Croatia">Toronto Croatia</a> from 1956 until they merged with the NASL's Toronto Metros in 1975, and then again after they sold-out of the NASL in 1979.</i></p>
<p>Of the 67 teams that played in the NASL over the course of its 17 seasons, many represent relocated franchises, and a handful represent the same franchise in the same location with changed names such as the Apollos, Cosmos and Earthquakes. The total number of unique clubs was 43.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Teams_that_played_indoor_seasons_.281971.2C_1975.E2.80.9376.2C_1979.E2.80.9384.29">Teams that played indoor seasons (1971, 1975–76, 1979–84)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_Soccer_League_(1968%E2%80%9384)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Teams that played indoor seasons (1971, 1975–76, 1979–84)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commissioners">Commissioners</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_Soccer_League_(1968%E2%80%9384)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Commissioners">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>1967: <a href="/wiki/Dick_Walsh_(executive)" title="Dick Walsh (executive)">Dick Walsh</a> (USA) – After 18 years with the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Dodgers" title="Los Angeles Dodgers">Los Angeles Dodgers</a>, he was chosen to serve as commissioner of first the <a href="/wiki/United_Soccer_Association" title="United Soccer Association">United Soccer Association (USA)</a> in 1966, then the North American Soccer League (NASL), which resulted from the merger of the USA and the <a href="/wiki/National_Professional_Soccer_League_(1967)" title="National Professional Soccer League (1967)">National Professional Soccer League (NPSL)</a> prior to the 1968 season. He served the NASL through its first full season, 1968, then returned to baseball.</li>
<li>1967: Ken Macker (NPSL)</li>
<li>1968: Walsh and Macker co-commissioners</li>
<li>1969–83: <a href="/wiki/Phil_Woosnam" title="Phil Woosnam">Phil Woosnam</a> – He is credited as an important factor in the development of the NASL, and had been a major figure in promoting the league and had secured TV contracts from <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a> and <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-removal_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-removal-47">[47]</a></sup> He played a key role during 1970 in recruiting executives at <a href="/wiki/Warner_Communications" title="Warner Communications">Warner Communications</a> to invest in an expansion team—the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Cosmos_(1970%E2%80%9385)" title="New York Cosmos (1970–85)">New York Cosmos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Woosnam oversaw the westward expansion of NASL in the early 1970s, establishing teams in Los Angeles, the Bay Area, Seattle, and Vancouver. However, he also guided the league into several poor business decisions, such as over-expansion to 24 teams, and the league's owners had been losing significant money<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-removal_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-removal-47">[47]</a></sup> and was removed from his duties as commissioner of the NASL in 1983 following a vote of the club owners.</li>
<li>1983–84: <a href="/wiki/Howard_J._Samuels" title="Howard J. Samuels">Howard J. Samuels</a> – His pioneering methods in the <a href="/wiki/Petrochemical_industry" title="Petrochemical industry">petrochemical industry</a> and success in the then niche household <a href="/wiki/Consumerism" title="Consumerism">consumer</a> market translated into posts as Vice President of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mobil_Oil_Corporation" title="Mobil Oil Corporation">Mobil Oil Corporation</a>, Commissioner of the North American Soccer League, and Chairman to Elms Capital Management, <a href="/wiki/Management_Consulting_Group" title="Management Consulting Group">Alexander Proudfoot PLC</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Communities_In_Schools" title="Communities In Schools">Communities In Schools</a>.</li>
<li>1984–85: <a href="/wiki/Clive_Toye" title="Clive Toye">Clive Toye</a> (acting) – After the sudden death of <a href="/wiki/Howard_J._Samuels" title="Howard J. Samuels">Howard J. Samuels</a>, Toye was appointed interim president of the NASL in December 1984.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The league ceased operations the following Spring.</li>
</ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Teams_named_after_NASL_teams">Teams named after NASL teams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_Soccer_League_(1968%E2%80%9384)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Teams named after NASL teams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The current <a href="/wiki/Heritage_Cup_(MLS)" title="Heritage Cup (MLS)">Heritage Cup</a> in <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Soccer" title="Major League Soccer">Major League Soccer</a> was developed as a way to remember the NASL's heritage by having teams named after NASL teams to participate for a special trophy. Today, two MLS teams, San Jose and Seattle, play for this trophy, although Portland and Vancouver are both eligible for the trophy if they decide to participate in this derby.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Players">Players</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_Soccer_League_(1968%E2%80%9384)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Players">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:North_American_Soccer_League_(1968%E2%80%9384)_players" title="Category:North American Soccer League (1968–84) players">Category:North American Soccer League (1968–84) players</a></div>
<p>The NASL brought some of the world's best soccer players to the United States. The trend started early as players such as <a href="/wiki/Vav%C3%A1" title="Vavá">Vavá</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_McParland" title="Peter McParland">Peter McParland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rub%C3%A9n_Marino_Navarro" title="Rubén Marino Navarro">Rubén Marino Navarro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Co_Prins" title="Co Prins">Co Prins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Juan_Santisteban" title="Juan Santisteban">Juan Santisteban</a> appeared in the league in 1968. However after the Cosmos signed Pele in 1975, the number of famous names increased during the NASL's peak during the late 1970s and early 1980s. In fact, 20 of the 44 <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup_awards" title="FIFA World Cup awards">World Cup Best XI</a> selections between 1966 and 1978 spent time in the NASL. At one time NASL squads fielded the captains of the past three World Cup-winning teams—Beckenbauer (1974), Alberto (1970), and Moore (1966). Of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ballon_d%27Or_(1956%E2%80%932009)" title="Ballon d'Or (1956–2009)">European Footballer of the Year</a> awards from 1965 to 1976, eight of the twelve awards—Eusébio (1965), Best (1968), Muller (1970), Cruyff (1971, '73, '74), Beckenbauer (1972, '76) —were given to players who went on to play in NASL. In addition, several players went on to greater acclaim after leaving the NASL, among them <a href="/wiki/Peter_Beardsley" title="Peter Beardsley">Peter Beardsley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Grobbelaar" title="Bruce Grobbelaar">Bruce Grobbelaar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julio_C%C3%A9sar_Romero" title="Julio César Romero">Julio César Romero</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hugo_S%C3%A1nchez" title="Hugo Sánchez">Hugo Sánchez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Graeme_Souness" title="Graeme Souness">Graeme Souness</a>. Two players appeared in both the NASL and <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Soccer" title="Major League Soccer">MLS</a>, spanning a 12-year gap in North American professional soccer: Hugo Sánchez and <a href="/wiki/Roy_Wegerle" title="Roy Wegerle">Roy Wegerle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witzig2006_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Witzig2006-50">[50]</a></sup></p>

</table>
<p><i>*Cosmos dropped "New York" from name for 1977 and 1978 seasons</i></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Single-game_attendance_records">Single-game attendance records</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_Soccer_League_(1968%E2%80%9384)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Single-game attendance records">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The New York Cosmos hold 21 of the 24 <a href="/wiki/Record_attendances_in_United_States_club_soccer" title="Record attendances in United States club soccer">top attendance records</a> in NASL history. Of the 107 games involving NASL clubs that have drawn 40,000+ fans, 65 were Cosmos' home matches at Giants Stadium (excludes <a href="/wiki/Soccer_Bowl_%2778" title="Soccer Bowl '78">Soccer Bowl '78</a>). The table below ranks teams by the number of 40,000+ crowds they attracted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable sortable">
<tr>
<th>Team</th>
<th>40,000+</th>
<th>Highest Single Attendance</th>
<th>Notes</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>New York Cosmos</td>
<td>65 matches</td>
<td>77,691 <i>vs Ft. Lauderdale</i> (1977)</td>
<td>playoff game</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Tampa Bay Rowdies</td>
<td>12 matches</td>
<td>56,389 <i>vs California</i> (1980)</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Independence_Day_(United_States)" title="Independence Day (United States)">Fourth of July</a> fireworks display after game</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Minnesota Kicks</td>
<td>8 matches</td>
<td>49,572 <i>vs San Jose</i> (1976)</td>
<td>playoff game</td>
</tr>
<tr>

<td>4 matches</td>
